| 1849 | [Hippolyte Fizeau](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hippolyte_Fizeau "Hippolyte Fizeau"), toothed wheel | 315 000 000[[109]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speed_of_light#cite_note-How-111) | +5.1% |
| 1862 | [Léon Foucault](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/L%C3%A9on_Foucault "Léon Foucault"), rotating mirror | 298 000 000±500 000[[109]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speed_of_light#cite_note-How-111) | −0.60% |
| 1875 | Werner Siemens | 260 000 000[[128]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speed_of_light#cite_note-130) | −13.3% |
| 1893 | [Heinrich Hertz](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heinrich_Hertz "Heinrich Hertz") | 200 000 000[[129]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speed_of_light#cite_note-131) | −33.3% |
| 1907 | Rosa and Dorsey, EM constants | 299 710 000±30 000[[113]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speed_of_light#cite_note-Essen1948-115)[[114]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speed_of_light#cite_note-RosaDorsey-116) | −280[ppm](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Parts_per_million "Parts per million") |
| 1926 | [Albert A. Michelson](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Albert_A._Michelson "Albert A. Michelson"), rotating mirror | 299 796 000±4000[[130]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speed_of_light#cite_note-132) | +12 ppm |
| 1950 | Essen and Gordon-Smith, cavity resonator | 299 792 500±3000[[116]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speed_of_light#cite_note-Essen1950-118) | +0.14 ppm |
| 1958 | K. D. Froome, radio interferometry | 299 792 500±100[[120]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speed_of_light#cite_note-Froome1858-122) | +0.14 ppm |
| 1972 | Evenson _et al._, laser interferometry | 299 792 456.2±1.1[[122]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speed_of_light#cite_note-NIST_heterodyne-124) | −0.006 ppm |
| 1983 | 17th CGPM, definition of the metre | 299 792 458 (exact)[[100]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speed_of_light#cite_note-Resolution_1-102) | —N/a |

### Early history

[Empedocles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Empedocles "Empedocles") (c. 490–430 BCE) was the first to propose a theory of light[[131]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speed_of_light#cite_note-133) and claimed that light has a finite speed.[[132]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speed_of_light#cite_note-134) He maintained that light was something in motion, and therefore must take some time to travel. [Aristotle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aristotle "Aristotle") argued, to the contrary, that "light is due to the presence of something, but it is not a movement".[[133]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speed_of_light#cite_note-Statistics-135)[Euclid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Euclid "Euclid") and [Ptolemy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ptolemy "Ptolemy") advanced Empedocles' [emission theory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Emission_theory_(vision) "Emission theory (vision)") of vision, where light is emitted from the eye, thus enabling sight. Based on that theory, [Heron of Alexandria](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heron_of_Alexandria "Heron of Alexandria") argued that the speed of light must be [infinite](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Infinity "Infinity") because distant objects such as stars appear immediately upon opening the eyes.[[134]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speed_of_light#cite_note-136)

[Early Islamic philosophers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Early_Islamic_philosophy "Early Islamic philosophy") initially agreed with the [Aristotelian view](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aristotelian_physics "Aristotelian physics") that light had no speed of travel. In 1021, [Alhazen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alhazen "Alhazen") (Ibn al-Haytham) published the _[Book of Optics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Book\_of\_Optics "Book of Optics")_, in which he presented a series of arguments dismissing the emission theory of [vision](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Visual_perception "Visual perception") in favour of the now accepted intromission theory, in which light moves from an object into the eye.[[135]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speed_of_light#cite_note-137) This led Alhazen to propose that light must have a finite speed,[[133]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speed_of_light#cite_note-Statistics-135)[[136]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speed_of_light#cite_note-Hamarneh-138)[[137]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speed_of_light#cite_note-Lester-139) and that the speed of light is variable, decreasing in denser bodies.[[137]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speed_of_light#cite_note-Lester-139)[[138]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speed_of_light#cite_note-140) He argued that light is substantial matter, the propagation of which requires time, even if this is hidden from the senses.[[139]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speed_of_light#cite_note-141) Also in the 11th century, [Abū Rayhān al-Bīrūnī](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Al-Biruni "Al-Biruni") agreed that light has a finite speed, and observed that the speed of light is much faster than the speed of sound.[[140]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speed_of_light#cite_note-142)

In the 13th century, [Roger Bacon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roger_Bacon "Roger Bacon") argued that the speed of light in air was not infinite, using philosophical arguments backed by the writing of Alhazen and Aristotle.[[141]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speed_of_light#cite_note-Lindberg-143)[[142]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speed_of_light#cite_note-144) In the 1270s, [Witelo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Witelo "Witelo") considered the possibility of light travelling at infinite speed in vacuum, but slowing down in denser bodies.[[143]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speed_of_light#cite_note-Marshall-145)

In the early 17th century, [Johannes Kepler](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Johannes_Kepler "Johannes Kepler") believed that the speed of light was infinite since empty space presents no obstacle to it. [René Descartes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Descartes "René Descartes") argued that if the speed of light were to be finite, the Sun, Earth, and Moon would be noticeably out of alignment during a [lunar eclipse](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lunar_eclipse "Lunar eclipse"). Although this argument fails when aberration of light is taken into account, the latter was not recognized until the following century.[[144]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speed_of_light#cite_note-146) Since such misalignment had not been observed, Descartes concluded the speed of light was infinite. Descartes speculated that if the speed of light were found to be finite, his whole system of philosophy might be demolished.[[133]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speed_of_light#cite_note-Statistics-135) Despite this, in his derivation of [Snell's law](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Snell%27s_law "Snell's law"), Descartes assumed that some kind of motion associated with light was faster in denser media.[[145]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speed_of_light#cite_note-147)[[146]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speed_of_light#cite_note-148)[Pierre de Fermat](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pierre_de_Fermat "Pierre de Fermat") derived Snell's law using the opposing assumption, the denser the medium the slower light travelled. Fermat also argued in support of a finite speed of light.[[147]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speed_of_light#cite_note-149)

### First measurement attempts

In 1629, [Isaac Beeckman](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Isaac_Beeckman "Isaac Beeckman") proposed an experiment in which a person observes the flash of a cannon reflecting off a mirror about one mile (1.6 km) away. In 1638, [Galileo Galilei](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galileo_Galilei "Galileo Galilei") proposed an experiment, with an apparent claim to having performed it some years earlier, to measure the speed of light by observing the delay between uncovering a lantern and its perception some distance away. He was unable to distinguish whether light travel was instantaneous or not, but concluded that if it were not, it must nevertheless be extraordinarily rapid.[[123]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speed_of_light#cite_note-2newsciences-125)[[124]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speed_of_light#cite_note-boyer-126) According to Galileo, the lanterns he used were "at a short distance, less than a mile". Assuming the distance was not too much shorter than a mile, and that "about a thirtieth of a second is the minimum time interval distinguishable by the unaided eye", Boyer notes that Galileo's experiment could at best be said to have established a lower limit of about 60 miles per second for the velocity of light.[[124]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speed_of_light#cite_note-boyer-126) In 1667, the [Accademia del Cimento](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Accademia_del_Cimento "Accademia del Cimento") of Florence reported that it had performed Galileo's experiment, with the lanterns separated by about one mile, but no delay was observed.[[148]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speed_of_light#cite_note-150) The actual delay in this experiment would have been about 11 [microseconds](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Microsecond "Microsecond").

[![Image 21: A diagram of a planet's orbit around the Sun and of a moon's orbit around another planet. The shadow of the latter planet is shaded.](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Illustration_from_1676_article_on_Ole_R%C3%B8mer%27s_measurement_of_the_speed_of_light.png/250px-Illustration_from_1676_article_on_Ole_R%C3%B8mer%27s_measurement_of_the_speed_of_light.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Illustration_from_1676_article_on_Ole_R%C3%B8mer%27s_measurement_of_the_speed_of_light.png)

Rømer's observations of the occultations of Io from Earth

[The first quantitative estimate of the speed of light](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/R%C3%B8mer%27s_determination_of_the_speed_of_light "Rømer's determination of the speed of light") was made in 1676 by Ole Rømer.[[101]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speed_of_light#cite_note-cohen-103)[[102]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speed_of_light#cite_note-roemer-104) From the observation that the periods of Jupiter's innermost moon [Io](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Io_(moon) "Io (moon)") appeared to be shorter when the Earth was approaching Jupiter than when receding from it, he concluded that light travels at a finite speed, and estimated that it takes light 22 minutes to cross the diameter of Earth's orbit. [Christiaan Huygens](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Christiaan_Huygens "Christiaan Huygens") combined this estimate with an estimate for the diameter of the Earth's orbit to obtain an estimate of speed of light of 220 000 km/s, which is 27% lower than the actual value.[[127]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speed_of_light#cite_note-Huygens_1690_8%E2%80%939-129)

In his 1704 book _[Opticks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Opticks "Opticks")_, [Isaac Newton](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Isaac_Newton "Isaac Newton") reported Rømer's calculations of the finite speed of light and gave a value of "seven or eight minutes" for the time taken for light to travel from the Sun to the Earth (the modern value is 8 minutes 19 seconds).[[149]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speed_of_light#cite_note-151) Newton queried whether Rømer's eclipse shadows were coloured. Hearing that they were not, he concluded the different colours travelled at the same speed. In 1729, [James Bradley](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/James_Bradley "James Bradley") discovered [stellar aberration](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aberration_of_light "Aberration of light").[[103]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speed_of_light#cite_note-Bradley1729-105) From this effect he determined that light must travel 10,210 times faster than the Earth in its orbit (the modern figure is 10,066 times faster) or, equivalently, that it would take light 8 minutes 12 seconds to travel from the Sun to the Earth.[[103]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speed_of_light#cite_note-Bradley1729-105)

### Connections with electromagnetism

See also: [History of electromagnetic theory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_electromagnetic_theory "History of electromagnetic theory") and [History of special relativity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_special_relativity "History of special relativity")

In the 19th century [Hippolyte Fizeau](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hippolyte_Fizeau "Hippolyte Fizeau") developed a method to determine the speed of light based on time-of-flight measurements on Earth and reported a value of 315 000 km/s.[[150]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speed_of_light#cite_note-guarnieri_7-1-152) His method was improved upon by [Léon Foucault](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/L%C3%A9on_Foucault "Léon Foucault") who obtained a value of 298 000 km/s in 1862.[[109]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speed_of_light#cite_note-How-111) In the year 1856, [Wilhelm Eduard Weber](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wilhelm_Eduard_Weber "Wilhelm Eduard Weber") and [Rudolf Kohlrausch](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rudolf_Kohlrausch "Rudolf Kohlrausch") measured the ratio of the electromagnetic and electrostatic units of charge, 1/√_ε_ 0 _μ_ 0, by discharging a [Leyden jar](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Leyden_jar "Leyden jar"), and found that its numerical value was very close to the speed of light as measured directly by Fizeau. The following year [Gustav Kirchhoff](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gustav_Kirchhoff "Gustav Kirchhoff") calculated that an electric signal in a [resistanceless](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electrical_resistance "Electrical resistance") wire travels along the wire at this speed.[[151]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speed_of_light#cite_note-153)

In the early 1860s, Maxwell showed that, according to the theory of electromagnetism he was working on, electromagnetic waves propagate in empty space[[152]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speed_of_light#cite_note-154) at a speed equal to the above Weber/Kohlrausch ratio, and drawing attention to the numerical proximity of this value to the speed of light as measured by Fizeau, he proposed that light is in fact an electromagnetic wave.[[153]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speed_of_light#cite_note-maxwellbio-155) Maxwell backed up his claim with his own experiment published in the 1868 Philosophical Transactions which determined the ratio of the electrostatic and electromagnetic units of electricity.[[154]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speed_of_light#cite_note-156)

### "Luminiferous aether"

Main article: [Luminiferous aether](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Luminiferous_aether "Luminiferous aether")

The wave properties of light were well known since [Thomas Young](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thomas_Young_(scientist) "Thomas Young (scientist)"). In the 19th century, physicists believed light was propagating in a medium called aether (or ether). After Maxwell's theory unified light and electric and magnetic waves, it was favored that both light and electric magnetic waves propagate in the same aether medium (or called the [luminiferous aether](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Luminiferous_aether "Luminiferous aether")).[[155]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speed_of_light#cite_note-157)

[![Image 22](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Einstein_en_Lorentz.jpg/250px-Einstein_en_Lorentz.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Einstein_en_Lorentz.jpg)

Hendrik Lorentz (right) with Albert Einstein (1921)

Some physicists thought that this aether acted as a [preferred frame](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Preferred_frame "Preferred frame") of reference for the propagation of light and therefore it should be possible to measure the motion of the Earth with respect to this medium, by measuring the [isotropy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Isotropy "Isotropy") of the speed of light. Beginning in the 1880s several experiments were performed to try to detect this motion, the most famous of which is [the experiment](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Michelson%E2%80%93Morley_experiment "Michelson–Morley experiment") performed by [Albert A. Michelson](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Albert_A._Michelson "Albert A. Michelson") and [Edward W. Morley](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Edward_W._Morley "Edward W. Morley") in 1887.[[156]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speed_of_light#cite_note-158)[[157]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speed_of_light#cite_note-159) The detected motion was found to always be nil (within observational error). Modern experiments indicate that the two-way speed of light is [isotropic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Isotropic "Isotropic") (the same in every direction) to within 6 nanometres per second.[[158]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speed_of_light#cite_note-160)

Because of Michelson-Morley experiment [Hendrik Lorentz](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hendrik_Lorentz "Hendrik Lorentz") proposed that the motion of the apparatus through the aether may cause the apparatus to [contract](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lorentz_contraction "Lorentz contraction") along its length in the direction of motion, and he further assumed that the time variable for moving systems must also be changed accordingly ("local time"), which led to the formulation of the [Lorentz transformation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lorentz_transformation "Lorentz transformation"). Based on [Lorentz's aether theory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lorentz_ether_theory "Lorentz ether theory"), [Henri Poincaré](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Henri_Poincar%C3%A9 "Henri Poincaré") (1900) showed that this local time (to first order in _v_/_c_) is indicated by clocks moving in the aether, which are synchronized under the assumption of constant light speed. In 1904, he speculated that the speed of light could be a limiting velocity in dynamics, provided that the assumptions of Lorentz's theory are all confirmed. In 1905, Poincaré brought Lorentz's aether theory into full observational agreement with the [principle of relativity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Principle_of_relativity "Principle of relativity").[[159]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speed_of_light#cite_note-161)[[160]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speed_of_light#cite_note-162)

### Special relativity

In 1905 Einstein postulated from the outset that the speed of light in vacuum, measured by a non-accelerating observer, is independent of the motion of the source or observer. Using this and the principle of relativity as a basis he derived the [special theory of relativity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Special_theory_of_relativity "Special theory of relativity"), in which the speed of light in vacuum c featured as a fundamental constant, also appearing in contexts unrelated to light. This made the concept of the stationary aether (to which Lorentz and Poincaré still adhered) useless and revolutionized the concepts of space and time.[[161]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speed_of_light#cite_note-163)[[162]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speed_of_light#cite_note-164)

### Increased accuracy of c and redefinition of the metre and second

See also: [History of the metre](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_metre "History of the metre")

In the second half of the 20th century, much progress was made in increasing the accuracy of measurements of the speed of light, first by cavity resonance techniques and later by laser interferometer techniques. These were aided by new, more precise, definitions of the metre and second. In 1950, [Louis Essen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Louis_Essen "Louis Essen") determined the speed as 299 792.5±3.0 km/s, using cavity resonance.[[116]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speed_of_light#cite_note-Essen1950-118) This value was adopted by the 12th General Assembly of the Radio-Scientific Union in 1957. In 1960, the [metre was redefined](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_metre#Krypton_standard "History of the metre") in terms of the wavelength of a particular spectral line of [krypton-86](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Krypton-86 "Krypton-86"), and, in 1967, the second was redefined in terms of the hyperfine transition frequency of the ground state of [caesium-133](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Caesium-133 "Caesium-133").[[163]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speed_of_light#cite_note-13thCGPMr1-165)

In 1972, using the laser interferometer method and the new definitions, a group at the US [National Bureau of Standards](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_Institute_of_Standards_and_Technology "National Institute of Standards and Technology") in [Boulder, Colorado](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Boulder,_Colorado "Boulder, Colorado") determined the speed of light in vacuum to be c = 299 792 456.2±1.1 m/s. This was 100 times less uncertain than the previously accepted value. The remaining uncertainty was mainly related to the definition of the metre.[[164]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speed_of_light#cite_note-11thCGPM-166)[[122]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speed_of_light#cite_note-NIST_heterodyne-124) As similar experiments found comparable results for c, the 15th [General Conference on Weights and Measures](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/General_Conference_on_Weights_and_Measures "General Conference on Weights and Measures") in 1975 recommended using the value 299 792 458 m/s for the speed of light.[[165]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speed_of_light#cite_note-15thCGPM-167)

### Defined as an explicit constant

In 1983 the 17th meeting of the General Conference on Weights and Measures (CGPM) found that wavelengths from frequency measurements and a given value for the speed of light are more [reproducible](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reproducibility "Reproducibility") than the previous standard. They kept the 1967 definition of second, so the [caesium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Caesium "Caesium")[hyperfine frequency](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hyperfine_structure#Use_in_defining_the_SI_second_and_meter "Hyperfine structure") would now determine both the second and the metre. To do this, they redefined the metre as "the length of the path traveled by light in vacuum during a time interval of 1/299 792 458 of a second".[[100]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speed_of_light#cite_note-Resolution_1-102)

As a result of this definition, the value of the speed of light in vacuum is exactly 299 792 458 m/s[[166]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speed_of_light#cite_note-Wheeler-168)[[167]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speed_of_light#cite_note-timeline-169) and has become a defined constant in the SI system of units.[[16]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speed_of_light#cite_note-fixes-18) Improved experimental techniques that, prior to 1983, would have measured the speed of light no longer affect the known value of the speed of light in SI units, but instead allow a more precise realization of the metre by more accurately measuring the wavelength of krypton-86 and other light sources.[[168]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speed_of_light#cite_note-Adams-170)[[169]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speed_of_light#cite_note-W_Rindler-171)

In 2011, the CGPM stated its intention to redefine all seven SI base units using what it calls "the explicit-constant formulation", where each "unit is defined indirectly by specifying explicitly an exact value for a well-recognized fundamental constant", as was done for the speed of light. It proposed a new, but completely equivalent, wording of the metre's definition: "The metre, symbol m, is the unit of length; its magnitude is set by fixing the numerical value of the speed of light in vacuum to be equal to exactly 299 792 458 when it is expressed in the SI unit m s−1."[[170]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speed_of_light#cite_note-172) This was one of the changes that was incorporated in the [2019 revision of the SI](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2019_revision_of_the_SI "2019 revision of the SI"), also termed the _New SI_.[[171]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speed_of_light#cite_note-173)

12.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speed_of_light#cite_ref-Yc_14-0)**Gibbs, P. (2004) [1997]. ["Why is _c_ the symbol for the speed of light?"](https://web.archive.org/web/20100325220247/http://math.ucr.edu/home/baez/physics/Relativity/SpeedOfLight/c.html). _Usenet Physics FAQ_. [University of California, Riverside](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/University_of_California,_Riverside "University of California, Riverside"). Archived from [the original](http://math.ucr.edu/home/baez/physics/Relativity/SpeedOfLight/c.html) on 25 March 2010. Retrieved 16 November 2009. "The origins of the letter c being used for the speed of light can be traced back to a paper of 1856 by Weber and Kohlrausch [...] Weber apparently meant c to stand for 'constant' in his force law, but there is evidence that physicists such as Lorentz and Einstein were accustomed to a common convention that c could be used as a variable for velocity. This usage can be traced back to the classic Latin texts in which c stood for 'celeritas', meaning 'speed'."

102.   ^ [_**a**_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speed_of_light#cite_ref-roemer_104-0)[_**b**_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speed_of_light#cite_ref-roemer_104-1)[_**c**_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speed_of_light#cite_ref-roemer_104-2)["Demonstration tovchant le mouvement de la lumiere trouvé par M. Rŏmer de l'Académie Royale des Sciences"](http://www-obs.univ-lyon1.fr/labo/fc/ama09/pages_jdsc/pages/jdsc_1676_lumiere.pdf) [Demonstration to the movement of light found by Mr. Römer of the Royal Academy of Sciences] (PDF). _[Journal des sçavans](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Journal\_des\_s%C3%A7avans "Journal des sçavans")_ (in French): 233–236. 1676.

Translated in ["A demonstration concerning the motion of light, communicated from Paris, in the Journal des Sçavans, and here made English"](https://doi.org/10.1098%2Frstl.1677.0024). _[Philosophical Transactions of the Royal Society](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Philosophical\_Transactions\_of\_the\_Royal\_Society "Philosophical Transactions of the Royal Society")_. **12** (136): 893–895. 1677. [Bibcode](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bibcode_(identifier) "Bibcode (identifier)"):[1677RSPT...12..893.](https://ui.adsabs.harvard.edu/abs/1677RSPT...12..893.). [doi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Doi_(identifier) "Doi (identifier)"):[10.1098/rstl.1677.0024](https://doi.org/10.1098%2Frstl.1677.0024).

Reproduced in Hutton, C.; Shaw, G.; Pearson, R., eds. (1809). ["On the Motion of Light by M. Romer"](https://archive.org/stream/philosophicaltra02royarich#page/397/mode/1up). _The Philosophical Transactions of the Royal Society of London, from Their Commencement in 1665, in the Year 1800: Abridged_. Vol.II. From 1673 to 1682. London: C. & R. Baldwin. pp.397–398.

 The account published in _Journal des sçavans_ was based on a report that Rømer read to the [French Academy of Sciences](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/French_Academy_of_Sciences "French Academy of Sciences") in November 1676 [(Cohen, 1940, p.346)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speed_of_light#cohen-1940).

# [Physics](/explore/ology/physics) > [Train of Thought](/explore/ology/physics/train-of-thought2) > [Light Speed](/explore/ology/physics/train-of-thought2/light-speed)

Light Speed

!["What if one were to run after a ray of light or were riding on the beam? If one were to run fast enough, would it no longer move at all?" by Einstein]()

**Light is fast! It can reach the universal speed limit — 186,000 miles per second.** (If you could travel as fast as light, the [universe](/explore/ology/ology-cards/083-universe?view=modal)
would look very different.) Because it moves so quickly, light can seem to appear instantaneously. Think about when you turn on a TV... images pop up right away.

How does the speed of [light](/explore/ology/ology-cards/166-light?view=modal)
affect our lives and what we know about the universe? **To shed some light on the subject, power up this thought experiment:**

Question: What if light could only travel 1 foot/second?

**Example:** Imagine you are watching a live concert broadcast on TV.

![outdoor summer concert with conductor conducting the orchestra]()

**Example:** Imagine you are watching a live concert broadcast on TV.

![outdoor summer concert with conductor conducting the orchestra]()

![Scenes of person watching Summer concert in July on tv. Left is 1 mile away from concert. Right is 3,000 miles away. They see concert instantaneously.]()

Logic: When light travels at its normal speed of 186,000 miles/second, you see the broadcast immediately. You could be 1 mile or 3,000 miles away from the concert — it doesn't matter! Why? Light can travel distances very rapidly. If you changed how fast light could travel, then you would change the amount of time it would take for it to reach your eyes.

**Prediction:** If [light](/explore/ology/ology-cards/166-light?view=modal)
could only travel 1 foot/second, the broadcast would reach viewers at different times, depending on their distance from the concert. Somebody sitting 1 mile away would receive the broadcast with a delay of over an hour. Somebody 3,000 miles away would receive the broadcast about 6 months later. Not exactly live TV!

![If light travelled 1ft per sec. the viewer 1 mi away would see the broadcast over an hour later but the 3,000 mi away would see it 6 months later!]()

**Conclusion:** The farther away you are from a source of light, the longer it takes the light to reach you.

**Did You Know?** Distances on Earth are relatively short, which is why live TV can be seen anywhere on [Earth](/explore/ology/ology-cards/050-earth?view=modal)
at almost exactly the same time. But distances in the universe are SO great that by the time light from an object in space, like a star, reaches our eyes, what we are seeing is a snapshot of how it looked hundreds or even millions of years ago!
